2:26:1542:14:14:72:107:686:12:Times:12:Times:?:Sorting
2:27:1549:14:14:72:367:672:12:Times:12:Times:?:Mohit Agrawal, Joseph G. Altonji, and Richard K. Mansfield

2:31:1677:28:28:273:338:602:12:Times:12:Times:?:ABSTRACT
2:32:1685:28:14:72:546:574:12:Times:12:Times:?:We  extend  the  control  function  approach  of  Altonji  and  Mansfield  (2018)  to  allow  for  multiple 
2:33:1793:14:14:72:545:560:12:Times:12:Times:?:group levels and complementarities. Our analysis provides a foundation for causal interpretation 
2:34:1889:14:14:72:546:546:12:Times:12:Times:?:of  multilevel mixed  effects  models  in  the  presence  of  sorting.  In  our  empirical  application,  we 
2:35:1997:14:14:71:546:532:12:Times:12:Times:?:obtain  lower  bound estimates  of  the  importance  of  school  and  commuting  zone  inputs  for 
2:36:2095:14:14:71:546:518:12:Times:12:Times:?:education  and  wages.  A  school/location combination  at  the  90th  versus  10th  percentile  of  the 
2:37:2199:14:14:71:545:504:12:Times:12:Times:?:school/location  quality  distribution  increases the  high  school  graduation  probability  and  college 
2:38:2305:14:14:71:545:490:12:Times:12:Times:?:enrollment  probability  by  at  least  .06  and  .17,  respectively. Treatment  effects  are  heterogeneous 
2:39:2413:14:42:71:476:476:12:Times:12:Times:?:across subgroups, primarily due to nonlinearity in the educational attainment model.

3:59:2909:0:27:91:190:732:14:g_d0_f3:14:g_d0_f3:C:1    Introduction
3:60:2926:27:17:107:523:705:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Designers of policy interventions often must choose whether to target neighborhoods, schools,
3:61:3019:17:16:91:523:688:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:or broader local or regional areas.  They would like to know which policies are most effective for
3:62:3117:16:17:91:523:672:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:each area, but research has been very slow in providing answers (Katz 2015). In this paper, we focus
3:63:3217:17:16:91:523:655:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on the more modest goal of measuring the overall importance of neighborhoods, schools, and local
3:64:3313:16:17:91:523:639:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:areas for adult outcomes. Information about where inequality arises provides guidance about where
3:65:3410:17:19:91:494:622:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the most potential progress can be made, even if it does not provide a specific plan of action.
3:66:3505:19:16:107:523:603:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The efficacy of particular policies and the relative importance of neighborhood vs.  school.  vs.
3:67:3602:16:17:91:523:587:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:broader local area factors is likely to vary across students.  For example, school-level dropout pre-
3:68:3703:17:16:91:523:570:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:vention policies may improve outcomes for struggling students but not stronger or better-supported
3:69:3801:16:17:91:523:554:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:students,  while  opening  a  satellite  campus  of  a  flagship  research  university  might  benefit  high
3:70:3909:17:16:91:523:537:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:achieving low income students the most.  Such considerations motivate us to work with a multi-
3:71:4003:16:16:91:523:521:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:level mixed effects (MME) model in which individual characteristics interact with observed and
3:72:4097:16:20:91:280:505:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unobserved school and area characteristics.
3:73:4140:20:16:107:523:485:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The use of MME models (also known as hierarchical linear models and random coefficients
3:74:4227:16:17:91:523:469:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:models) was stimulated by interest in the interplay among the student, classroom, and school factors
3:75:4327:17:16:91:523:452:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that determine educational outcomes.1However,  the MME literature is mindful of but does not
3:76:4419:16:16:91:523:436:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:address sorting bias arising from school and neighborhood choice. As Durlauf (2004) and Graham
3:77:4513:16:17:91:523:420:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(2018)  emphasize,  differences  in  outcomes  across  neighborhoods  combine  the  causal  effects  of
3:78:4616:17:16:91:523:403:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:neighborhood with differences across neighborhoods in the observed and unobserved attributes of
3:79:4711:16:20:91:498:387:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:children that matter for their outcomes. Sorting poses similar problems for assessing schools.
3:80:4805:20:16:107:523:367:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Our main methodological contribution is to address the sorting problem present in MME mod-
3:81:4895:16:17:91:523:351:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:els.  We do this by using a flexible production function specification and a rich but tractable model
3:82:4996:17:16:91:523:334:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of school and neighborhood choice to derive exact formulas relating the slope parameters and error
3:83:5094:16:16:91:523:318:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:component variances of a standard MME specification to the corresponding production function pa-
3:84:5190:16:17:91:523:302:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:rameters. We then use these formulas combined with MME estimates to characterize the importance
3:85:5285:17:19:91:490:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of school and location choices for students’ long-run education and labor market outcomes.
3:86:5375:19:17:107:523:266:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We postpone precise statements of our theoretical results and the assumptions, but the questions
3:87:5471:17:16:91:523:249:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:about MME models that we address and the answers that we give may be summarized as follows.
3:88:5562:16:36:91:324:233:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Consider the following MME level regression model:
3:89:5612:36:27:162:524:197:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f4:A:Ygi=XXXiBBB+XXXgGGG1+ZZZ2gGGG2+MiXXXgrrr1+MiZZZ2grrr2+vg+(vgi−vg).(1)
3:90:5681:27:16:107:523:170:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Hereidenotes the student andgdenotes the neighborhood, school, and associated local area
3:91:5769:16:17:91:523:154:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the student is exposed to.Ygiis a long-run student outcome,  such as wages at age 25.XXXiis a
3:92:5862:17:28:91:524:137:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:set of observed student characteristics such as mother’s education.XXXgis a set of observed peer
3:93:5958:28:14:107:523:109:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:1Garner and Raudenbush (1991) is a good example.  The early editions of Goldstein (2011) and Raudenbush and
3:94:6065:14:0:91:312:95:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:Bryk (2002) became key references for empirical researchers.
4:95:6126:0:16:91:524:732:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:characteristics—averages ofXXXiamong the students ing.ZZZ2gis a set of observed characteristics
4:96:6221:16:17:91:523:716:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of  the  school  and  location  that  influence  wages,  such  as  student/teacher  ratio.Miis  a  student
4:97:6327:17:16:91:523:699:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:characteristic that interacts withXXXgandZZZ2gin influencing outcomes, such as minority status.vg
4:98:6424:16:16:91:523:683:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is a group specific error and(vgi−vg)is an idiosyncratic error.   The error components are treated
4:99:6522:16:17:91:523:667:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:as random effects and are defined to be uncorrelated with the explanatory variables.   The slope
4:100:6618:17:16:91:523:650:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:coefficients and error variances are defined to be what one recovers using a random effects estimator
4:101:6719:16:20:91:201:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:with a very large sample.
4:102:6744:20:16:107:523:614:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The production function that actually determines the outcomeYgiis assumed to take the same
4:103:6834:16:16:91:523:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:linear form with interaction terms as the above estimating equation.However,  it also includes
4:104:6928:16:17:91:524:582:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unobserved  (by  the  econometrician)  counterparts  toXXXi,XXXg,ZZZ2g,MiXXXgandMiZZZ2g.   In  general,
4:105:7031:17:16:91:523:565:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:sorting-driven differences across school/locations in unobserved student characteristics as well as
4:106:7130:16:17:91:523:549:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:other forms of omitted variables bias lead the parameters of (1) to differ from the production function
4:107:7233:17:19:91:142:532:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:parameters.
4:108:7244:19:16:107:523:513:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We then build upon and extend to an interactive setting the control function approach of Altonji
4:109:7340:16:17:91:523:497:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and Mansfield (2018) (hereafter,  AM). In particular,  they show that even a flexible version of a
4:110:7438:17:16:91:523:480:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:standard multinomial choice model implies a relationship between school/neighborhood averages of
4:111:7534:16:17:91:523:464:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:observed student characteristics and averages ofunobservedstudent characteristics. Consequently,
4:112:7630:17:16:91:523:447:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school/neighborhood averages of observed student characteristics(XXXg)can serve as controls for the
4:113:7729:16:17:91:523:431:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:averages of unobserved student characteristics that would otherwise bias estimates of the importance
4:114:7829:17:19:91:247:414:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of school and neighborhood effects.
4:115:7864:19:16:107:523:395:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f4:A:Under somewhat stronger assumptions (discussed below) we show that the coefficient vectorrrr2
4:116:7957:16:17:91:523:379:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on the interaction between a student characteristicMisuch as mother’s education and a set of ob-
4:117:8053:17:16:91:523:362:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:served “group” characteristicsZZZ2gis the sum of two components. The first is the causal effect of the
4:118:8155:16:17:91:523:346:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:interaction between mother’s education and the observed group characteristics.  The second is the
4:119:8252:17:16:91:523:329:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:B:effect of the interaction between mother’s education and the part of the contribution ofunobserved
4:120:8350:16:17:91:523:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:group characteristics (e.g.  the quality of a principal) that covary with the observed group charac-
4:121:8450:17:16:91:523:296:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:teristics, holding observed and unobserved student characteristics fixed. Analogously,rrr1combines
4:122:8548:16:16:91:523:280:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:two components.  The first is the causal effect of interactions betweenMiand both observed and un-
4:123:8646:16:17:91:523:264:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:observed peer characteristics (such as average mother’s education and average unobserved parental
4:124:8743:17:16:91:523:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:motivation).  The second is the causal effect of part of the interaction betweenMiand unobserved
4:125:8839:16:17:91:523:231:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school and location characteristics.  The key point is that under the assumptions of the model the
4:126:8937:17:16:91:523:214:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:estimates of bothrrr1andrrr2exclusively reflect interactions between the student and the environment
4:127:9037:16:19:91:159:198:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and not sorting.
4:128:9053:19:17:107:523:179:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We also show that AM’s expression forGGG2, the coefficient vector on the main effects of observed
4:129:9150:17:16:91:523:162:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school and area characteristics, remains unchanged when observed and unobserved interactions are
4:130:9246:16:17:91:523:146:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:introduced to the production function.  Specifically,  it picks up the causal effect of the observed
4:131:9346:17:16:91:523:129:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school and area characteristics plus a second component that reflects their association with unob-
4:132:9444:16:17:91:523:113:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:served school and area characteristics that affect outcomes. It is not contaminated by sorting bias or
4:133:9546:17:0:91:523:96:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unobserved interactions.  AM’s result that school and neighborhood error componentsvgare simi-
5:134:9641:0:16:91:523:732:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:larly uncontaminated by the effects of sorting also carries over to the model with interaction effects.
5:135:9744:16:17:91:523:716:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Finally, we also justify a causal interpretation for a random slope coefficient onMithat captures
5:136:9841:17:16:91:523:699:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the interaction of an observed student characteristic with an index of unobserved school and area
5:137:9938:16:19:91:156:683:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:characteristics.
5:138:9954:19:17:107:523:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Taken together, these identification results reveal that even in the presence of endogenous sorting
5:139:10053:17:16:91:523:647:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of individuals to groups, multilevel mixed effects estimates can distinguish among four sources of
5:140:10151:16:17:91:523:631:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the variation in outcomes. The sources are individual contributions that are common across groups,
5:141:10249:17:16:91:523:614:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:group contributions that are common across individuals, contributions that consist of interactions
5:142:10347:16:16:91:524:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:between student and group inputs, and a set of ambiguous contributions (associated withXXXgGGG1)
5:143:10443:16:17:91:523:582:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that either reflect common group inputs or group-averages of individual inputs.  We then show that
5:144:10541:17:16:91:523:565:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:this decomposition is sufficient to generate meaningful (albeit conservative) answers to two key
5:145:10637:16:17:91:523:549:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:questions.   How much do the schools and locations we choose for our children matter for their
5:146:10731:17:16:91:523:532:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:outcomes?  And how much does the importance of schools and locations depend on the students’
5:147:10823:16:19:91:200:516:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and parents’ own inputs?
5:148:10847:19:17:107:523:497:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:A:As in AM’s analysis, the use of school/location averages of observed student characteristicsXXXg
5:149:10943:17:16:91:523:480:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to control for school/location averages of unobserved characteristics is likely to lead to an under-
5:150:11043:16:17:91:523:464:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:statement of the importance of school and neighborhood.  The main reason is that by treating the
5:151:11139:17:16:91:523:447:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:averages as controls that absorb sorting bias, we are discarding the main effects of peers on out-
5:152:11237:16:17:91:523:431:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:comes.  A second reason is that the group averages will absorb a portion of the unobserved school
5:153:11334:17:16:91:523:414:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:or area components that are uncorrelated with the observed components and are correlated with the
5:154:11431:16:17:91:523:398:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:amenities that families sort on. Consequently, our analysis only places a lower bound on the overall
5:155:11531:17:19:91:352:381:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:importance of school and area factors for student outcomes.
5:156:11590:19:16:107:523:362:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We use the above analysis as the theoretical foundation to interpret MME model estimates from
5:157:11683:16:17:91:523:346:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:two cohort-specific panel surveys, the National Educational Longitudinal Survey of 1988 (NELS88)
5:158:11779:17:16:91:523:329:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and the Educational Longitudinal Survey of 2002 (ELS2002). These data sets provide a rich set of
5:159:11875:16:17:91:523:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:student characteristics for samples of students from each of a large sample of schools. Students are
5:160:11975:17:16:91:523:296:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:followed for several years, which permits investigation of longer run outcomes such as high school
5:161:12073:16:16:91:523:280:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:graduation, attendance at a four year college, and attainment of a college degree.  In addition, they
5:162:12174:16:20:91:432:264:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:contain location of residence (ZIP code or block group) and school identifiers.
5:163:12253:20:16:107:523:244:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We use the multilevel model estimates to measure the consequences of exposure to a low-quality
5:164:12347:16:17:91:523:228:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school versus a high-quality school, and a low-quality commuting zone versus a high-quality com-
5:165:12443:17:16:91:523:211:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:muting zone.  Here we define “quality” narrowly as collections of attributes that contribute to the
5:166:12542:16:16:91:523:195:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:outcomes we consider (educational attainment or log wages).  Our main results concern the “treat-
5:167:12639:16:17:91:524:179:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ment effects” of 10th-to-90thpercentile shifts in school quality, in commuting zone quality, and in
5:168:12738:17:16:91:523:162:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:combined school and commuting zone quality.   First,  we consider average effects.   We take the
5:169:12834:16:17:91:523:146:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:average over the student population of the effect of a 10th-to-90thpercentile shift in the combined
5:170:12933:17:16:91:523:129:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school/commuting zone environment. Such a shift increases the high school graduation probability
5:171:13029:16:17:91:523:113:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:by 0.088 in NELS88 and by about 0.061 in ELS2002 (which has a higher baseline graduation rate–
5:172:13123:17:0:91:523:96:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:0.92).  The effect of the school treatment is 0.06 in NELS and about 0.044 in ELS. The values for
6:173:13221:0:19:91:469:732:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the 10th-to-90thquantile shift in commuting zone quality are between 0.039 and 0.067.
6:174:13306:19:16:107:523:713:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:For  the  college  enrollment  probability,  the  combined  school  and  commuting  zone  treatment
6:175:13405:16:17:91:523:697:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:effect is between 0.17 and 0.18 for both NELS and ELS. The values for the school treatment are
6:176:13499:17:16:91:523:680:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:about 0.125, while the values for the commuting zone treatment are about 0.115 in each dataset.
6:177:13594:16:17:91:523:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:The effects of the combined treatment, the school treatment and the commuting zone treatment on
6:178:13689:17:16:91:523:647:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the probabilility of completing a BA degree are somewhat smaller in both datasets (0.085, 0.077,
6:179:13785:16:17:91:523:631:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and 0.046 in NELS and around 0.085,  0.070,  and 0.065 in ELS). For wage rates at age 25,  the
6:180:13879:17:16:91:523:614:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:combined school and commuting zone treatment ranges between 11 and 12 percent. The values for
6:181:13972:16:16:91:523:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the school treatment range from 6.2 to 7.1 percent.  The commuting zone effects range from 7.8 to
6:182:14069:16:17:91:523:582:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:10.7 percent.  Overall, the results suggest that large changes in school and commuting zone inputs
6:183:14167:17:19:91:466:565:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:can make a substantial difference for students’ educational attainment and wage rates.
6:184:14253:19:17:107:523:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We also present estimates of school and commuting zone “treatment” effects for particular pop-
6:185:14347:17:16:91:523:529:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ulation groups:  blacks, Hispanics, whites with single mothers who did not attend college, whites
6:186:14444:16:16:91:523:513:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:with two resident parents with college degrees, as well as for students at each percentile of an index
6:187:14546:16:17:91:523:497:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of student level variables that predict the outcomes.  We do not find strong evidence that wages or
6:188:14645:17:16:91:523:480:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the latent indices governing the binary educational attainment outcomes depend on the interaction
6:189:14742:16:17:91:523:464:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:between observed student characteristics and characteristics of the school and commuting zone,
6:190:14836:17:16:91:523:447:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:although we cannot rule out modest interactions.  Nevertheless, we do find important differences
6:191:14932:16:17:91:523:431:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in treatment effects across students.  The heterogeneity is primarily due to the fact that the probit
6:192:15033:17:16:91:523:414:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:function that we use for the education outcomes naturally imposes greater sensitivity to school and
6:193:15132:16:17:91:523:398:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:location environment for students predicted to be close to the decision margin.  Specifically, high
6:194:15231:17:16:91:523:381:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school graduation rates of subpopulations that tend to be disadvantaged are particularly sensitive to
6:195:15332:16:16:91:523:365:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the external environment, while few students from advantaged subpopulations are near the margin.
6:196:15428:16:17:91:523:349:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:For college enrollment and college graduation, superior school and commuting zone inputs are im-
6:197:15524:17:16:91:523:332:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:portant for all, but particularly important for students near or above the middle of the distribution of
6:198:15628:16:20:91:229:316:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:student and family background.
6:199:15658:20:16:107:523:296:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Finally, we provide a richer picture of sorting than is possible with two level models featuring
6:200:15754:16:16:91:523:280:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:individual  and  school  (Coleman  et  al.   1966;  Jencks  and  Brown  1975;  Betts  1995;  Altonji  and
6:201:15859:16:17:91:523:264:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Mansfield 2011; Alexander and Morgan 2016) or individuals and neighborhood (Solon et al. 2000).
6:202:15954:17:16:91:523:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We find that segregation by student quality (defined by an index of characteristics that promote
6:203:16050:16:17:91:523:231:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:education and wages) is primarily across schools and commuting zones rather then within ZIP code
6:204:16146:17:16:91:523:214:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:or block group among schoolmates. Our variance decompositions of educational attainment and
6:205:16237:16:17:91:523:198:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wages consistently show that individual level factors account for the lion’s share of the variance,
6:206:16336:17:16:91:523:181:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:with school and commuting zone level variation making modest contributions, and neighborhood
6:207:16428:16:16:91:523:165:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:level factors playing a smaller role.  For example, in ELS2002 for college attendance, the within-
6:208:16526:16:17:91:523:149:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:neighborhood (block group) share is 71.4 percent, while the neighborhood share is 3.1 percent, the
6:209:16624:17:16:91:523:132:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school share is 15.9 percent, and the commuting zone share is 9.7 percent. School and commuting
6:210:16719:16:17:91:523:116:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:zone account for about 9 and 6 percent of the variance in log wages respectively, and block group
6:211:16816:17:0:91:523:99:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:within a school accounts for about 3%.  Within a school there is very little clustering in any of our
7:212:16918:0:19:91:228:732:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:outcomes at the ZIP code level.
7:213:16949:19:16:107:523:713:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Our paper builds on and contributes to several literatures.  The model of school and location
7:214:17042:16:17:91:523:697:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:choice that we use to address sorting on unobserved student characteristics is essentially that of
7:215:17140:17:16:91:523:680:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:AM,  although  we  are  more  specific  about  the  distinction  between  neighborhood  choice,  school
7:216:17243:16:17:91:524:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:choice, and region.2AM in turn draw on the rich theoretical and empirical literature on equilibrium
7:217:17342:17:16:91:523:647:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:sorting and matching other contexts.3Control function approaches are widely used in econometric
7:218:17437:16:19:91:116:631:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:work.
7:219:17442:19:17:107:523:612:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:On the empirical side,  the literatures on the importance of families,  neighborhoods,  schools,
7:220:17538:17:16:91:523:595:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and region are each too vast to discuss meaningfully here.  The most directly relevant are the large
7:221:17638:16:17:91:523:579:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:number of studies that descend from the Coleman Report’s (Coleman et al.   1966) examination
7:222:17730:17:16:91:523:562:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the importance of family background,  peer characteristics,  and school inputs using data with
7:223:17827:16:17:91:523:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a multilevel structure similar to the NELS88 and ELS2002.4They found,  in keeping with most
7:224:17918:17:16:91:523:529:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:subsequent research, that family background is by far the most important determinant of education
7:225:18015:16:16:91:523:513:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:success. Jencks and Brown (1975), Betts (1995), and Altonji and Mansfield (2011) are contributions
7:226:18113:16:17:91:523:497:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to the large literature in economics, sociology, and education that performs variance decompositions
7:227:18213:17:16:91:523:480:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:separating the contribution of school- or neighborhood-level factors versus student-level factors for
7:228:18314:16:17:91:523:464:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:test  scores,  educational  attainment,  and  in  a  few  cases  wage  rates.   Focusing  on  neighborhood,
7:229:18421:17:16:91:523:447:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Jencks and Mayer (1990) provide a comprehensive review of earlier studies from economics and
7:230:18513:16:17:91:523:431:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:sociology. They conclude that there is no strong evidence for neighborhood effects. However, some
7:231:18610:17:16:91:523:414:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the studies they summarize do find effects.  More recent reviews include Sampson et al.  (2002),
7:232:18709:16:17:91:523:398:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Durlauf (2004), Durlauf and Ioannides (2010), Harding et al.  (2011), Sharkey and Faber (2014),
7:233:18804:17:16:91:523:381:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and Graham (2018).5Many of the papers emphasize that estimates of the impacts of particular
7:234:18895:16:16:91:523:365:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:characteristics, such as percent minority, segregation measures, and poverty rates on socioeconomic
7:235:18994:16:17:91:523:349:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:outcomes of children, often by racial group and poverty status, are subject to the problem of sorting
7:236:19095:17:16:91:523:332:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:bias. A good example is Card and Rothstein (2007), who carefully address the issue in their analysis
7:237:19195:16:20:91:426:316:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the effects of segregation on the test score gap between blacks and whites.
7:238:19273:20:16:107:523:296:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Meghir and Rivkin (2011) discuss alternative approaches to estimating school fixed effects and
7:239:19367:16:16:91:523:280:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the effects of particular school inputs, and highlight the problem of endogenous selection of schools
7:240:19468:16:20:91:323:264:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and neighborhoods, among other econometric issues.
7:241:19518:20:16:107:523:244:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:There is also a small but growing experimental or quasi-experimental literature that attempts to
7:242:19614:16:17:91:523:228:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:measure neighborhood or school effects.  Oreopoulos (2003) and Jacob (2004) use quasi-random
7:243:19706:17:16:91:523:211:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:assignment of neighborhood in the wake of housing project closings to estimate the magnitude of
7:244:19801:16:19:91:523:195:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:neighborhood effects on student outcomes. They obtain small estimates, in contrast to Chyn (2018).
7:245:19899:19:14:107:523:176:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:2The multinomial choice formulation that we use to characterize the school/location choice problem with heteroge-
7:246:20012:14:11:91:523:162:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:nous preferences is standard in the consumer choice literature, drawing on McFadden (1984), Berry (1994), and many
7:247:20126:11:8:91:438:151:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:subsequent papers, including Bayer et al. (2007)’s study of the demand for housing and location.
7:248:20222:8:14:107:523:143:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:3These include marriage (eg., Browning et al.  2014), the labor market (eg., Rosen 1974; Ekeland et al.  2004; Lise
7:249:20337:14:8:91:474:129:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:2016; Lindenlaub 2017), and neighborhoods and schools (eg., Epple and Platt 1998; Epple and Sieg 1999).
7:250:20440:8:14:107:523:121:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:4See Alexander and Morgan (2016) and Lucas (2016) for a recent discussions of the legacy of the Coleman Report
7:251:20550:14:8:91:237:107:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:and its influence on subsequent research.
7:252:20591:8:0:107:383:99:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:A:5Kline and Moretti (2014) provide a recent analysis of placed based policies.
8:253:20669:0:16:91:523:732:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:A number of authors, including Kling et al.  (2007), use the Moving to Opportunity experiment to
8:254:20765:16:17:91:523:716:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:study effects of growing up in a low poverty neighborhood.  Most of the work finds small effects.
8:255:20862:17:16:91:523:699:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:However, Chetty et al.  (2016) revisit the MTO experiment using Internal Revenue Service data on
8:256:20958:16:16:91:523:683:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:young adult outcomes, including earnings, college attendance, and single parenthood.  They find
8:257:21053:16:20:91:453:667:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:substantial benefits for children who move before age 13 but not for older children.
8:258:21137:20:16:107:523:647:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Using a sibling differences approach that also exploits high quality tax data, Chetty and Hendren
8:259:21234:16:17:91:523:631:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(2018a; 2018b) identify county-level and commuting zone level neighborhood effects on earnings
8:260:21328:17:16:91:523:614:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that are larger than but qualitatively consistent with our results.  Aaronson (1998) finds substantial
8:261:21430:16:16:91:523:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:effects of the census tract-level poverty rate and high school dropout rate on dropout rates and years
8:262:21532:16:20:91:366:582:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f4:B:of education using a sibling differences design and PSID data.6
8:263:21595:20:16:107:523:562:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Bergman (2016) finds that a lottery based opportunity to transfer from a predominantly minority
8:264:21690:16:17:91:523:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school district to a high income predominantly white district increases college enrollment by 10
8:265:21786:17:16:91:523:529:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:percentage points. Deming et al. (2014) exploit randomized lottery outcomes from the school choice
8:266:21884:16:16:91:523:513:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:plan in the Charlotte-Mecklenburg district and find large effects of attending a chosen public school
8:267:21985:16:17:91:523:497:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on high school graduation, college enrollment, and college completion for students coming from low
8:268:22083:17:16:91:523:480:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:quality urban schools. Angrist et al. (2016) also use admissions lotteries. They find positive effects
8:269:22185:16:17:91:523:464:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of attending a Boston charter high school on performance on both high and low stakes exams and on
8:270:22282:17:16:91:523:447:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:attendance at four-year colleges relative to two-year colleges. On the other hand, Cullen et al. (2006)
8:271:22385:16:17:91:523:431:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:use a similar identification strategy with lotteries in Chicago Public Schools and do not find an effect
8:272:22489:17:16:91:523:414:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on the high school graduation probability.  Dobbie and Fryer (2011) and Laliberte (2018) are rare
8:273:22586:16:17:91:523:398:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:quasi-experimental attempts to distinguish neighborhood effects from school effects.  Dobbie and
8:274:22682:17:16:91:523:381:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Fryer (2011) finds that the Harlem Children’s Zone has little effect on test scores, while Promise
8:275:22780:16:16:91:523:365:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Academy Charter schools have large effects that do not vary with residence in the zone.  Laliberte
8:276:22878:16:17:91:523:349:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(2018) also finds that school effects are much more important than neighborhoods in determining
8:277:22973:17:19:91:366:332:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:postsecondary enrollment and completion, at least in Montreal.
8:278:23035:19:17:107:523:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The paper continues in Section 2, which presents the model of location and school choice and
8:279:23127:17:16:91:523:296:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:AM’s control function result. Section 3 presents the model of outcomes used in the paper. Section
8:280:23224:16:16:91:523:280:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:4 specifies the MME estimating equation used in the paper and establishes key identification results
8:281:23324:16:17:91:523:264:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:relating slope parameters and error terms of the estimating equation to the parameters of the un-
8:282:23421:17:16:91:523:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:derlying production function.  Section 5 discusses the data and variables used in the study.  Section
8:283:23522:16:17:91:523:231:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:6 presents the estimation methodology.  Section 7 presents the model estimates and the variance
8:284:23617:17:16:91:523:214:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:decompositions that are derived from them. Section 8 presents lower bound estimates of treatment
8:285:23713:16:17:91:523:198:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:effects on various educational outcomes and on log wages generated by shifts in school and com-
8:286:23808:17:72:91:496:181:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:muting zone quality, both on average and for particular subpopulations. Section 9 concludes.

22:770:62829:19:16:107:523:542:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:What about CZ-level unobservables represented byZZZCU2cΓΓΓCU2c? If the full set of commuting zone
22:771:62926:16:17:91:523:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:averages ofZZZN2nandZZZS2swere included inZZZC2c, then we could rule out the possibility that any unob-
22:772:63029:17:16:91:524:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:served commuting zone inputs could project onto eitherZZZS2norZZZS2sand be captured byGGGN2orGGGS2.
22:773:63128:16:17:91:523:493:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:This is because neighborhoods and schools are nested within commuting zones.  More generally,
22:774:63221:17:16:91:523:476:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:with nested levels, one can prevent unobservable inputs from being captured by regressors (includ-
22:775:63319:16:17:91:523:460:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ing interactions) associated with lower levels of aggregation by including the appropriate higher
22:776:63416:17:16:91:523:443:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:level averages of lower-level characteristics. Thus, with nested levels one could in principle place a
22:777:63518:16:16:91:523:427:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:lower bound on the relative importance of inputs at the lowest level (neighborhoods in our empirical
22:778:63618:16:17:91:523:411:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:work) and an upper bound on the relative importance of inputs at the highest level (CZs). We seek
22:779:63715:17:16:91:523:394:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to approximate this approach, though we cannot fully implement it due to concerns about overfitting
22:780:63814:16:39:91:284:378:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:given limited numbers of units at each level.

22:786:64235:17:16:91:523:237:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:cohort of American students.  The cohorts are students who were 8thgraders in 1988 for NELS88,

24:851:70039:20:16:107:523:456:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We do not use panel weights because we experienced convergence problems when we attempted

24:855:70425:16:17:91:523:391:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:third is because follow-up probabilities are based on outcomes.  The slope parameter estimates are

25:909:75467:8:14:103:523:153:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:22In preliminary work we experimented with a grouped backward stepwise regression procedure to pare down the
25:910:75575:14:11:91:523:139:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:variable sets at each unit level.  One could also consider other procedures, such as group lasso (Meier et al.  2008; Yuan
25:911:75697:11:11:91:523:128:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:and Lin 2006).   Ultimately,  we chose not to use these procedures because of uncertainty about how to do statistical

25:913:75938:11:11:91:523:106:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:variable selection and sampling error given variable choice, and about how to correct for finite sample bias in the variance
25:914:76062:11:0:91:389:95:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:estimates. The three step estimation method we use is computationally demanding.

26:925:76866:24:29:107:468:523:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We estimate the following restricted version of the MME estimating equation (22):

26:930:77255:16:17:91:523:409:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:First, we have excludedZZZN2nGGGN2and(MMMi⊗⊗⊗ZZZN2n)rrrN2because our only neighborhood-level variables

26:933:77553:17:16:91:523:359:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:This is because CZ is implicitly a part of the choice ofnands.  Thus, to prevent overly conserva-

26:936:77784:20:16:107:523:307:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Third,  to retain sufficient precision,  we have to reduce the dimensionality of the interactions

28:1065:84259:40:16:94:523:329:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:However, we do not adjust thercoefficients on the interaction terms (where we conjecture that the
28:1066:84356:16:20:91:469:313:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f4:B:sampling error in the indices might bias estimates of interaction effects toward zero).25
28:1067:84445:20:16:107:523:293:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Since the sampling error in the regression indices reflects true contributions of the error compo-
28:1068:84543:16:0:91:319:277:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:B:nents toYi, the flip side of upward bias inVar(XXXnˆGGG
28:1069:84598:0:10:319:324:284:8:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:?:N

28:1071:84616:0:10:376:380:284:8:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:?:S

28:1073:84629:0:7:419:423:281:8:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:?:S
28:1074:84630:7:0:419:435:274:8:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:?:2sˆGGG
28:1075:84636:0:10:435:439:284:8:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:?:S
28:1076:84637:10:0:435:496:274:8:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:?:2))), andVar(ZZZ
28:1077:84653:0:7:496:501:281:8:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:?:C
28:1078:84654:7:0:497:513:274:8:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:?:2cˆGGG
28:1079:84660:0:10:513:518:284:8:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:?:C
28:1080:84661:10:13:513:523:274:8:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f6:F:2)))
28:1081:84665:13:17:91:523:261:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is downward bias in the estimates ofVar(vc),Var(vs−vc),Var(vn−vs), andVar(vi−vn), which do
28:1082:84755:17:22:91:523:244:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:not account for degrees of freedom used in estimation.26We discuss how we allocate the bias ad-
28:1083:84850:22:14:103:523:222:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:24Specifically, we assign prior distributions from which our slope parameters and random effect variances are drawn,
28:1084:84966:14:11:91:523:208:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:and use Markov-chain Monte Carlo methods to estimate the means of the posterior distributions governing these parame-
28:1085:85083:11:11:91:523:197:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:ters. We use these posterior means in place of the fixed parameters defined in (23) when reporting results and computing
28:1086:85203:11:11:91:523:186:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:estimates of the impact of alternative group-level treatments below. The estimates are not very sensitive to modest changes
28:1087:85326:11:10:91:278:175:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:in the priors forVar(vc),Var(vs−vc),Var(vn−vs).
28:1088:85373:10:15:103:523:165:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:25We  estimatêVVV aaarrr(((ˆGGGS2)))using  the  formula  for  the  asymptotic  variance  of  the  Step  1  estimates.   We  account  for
28:1089:85510:15:11:91:523:150:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:clustering at the commuting zone level but not for the use of imputed data. We perform similar adjustments to the Step 2
28:1090:85630:11:8:91:264:139:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:estimates of the other index standard deviations.
28:1091:85679:8:14:103:523:131:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:26The same issue arises in linear regression with one normally distributed error component.  In this case, the MLE
28:1092:85793:14:11:91:523:117:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:estimator of the error variance is the sum of squared residuals divided by the sample size. The unbiased estimator adjusts
28:1093:85915:11:11:91:523:106:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:for degrees of freedom by dividing by the sample size minus 1 plus the number of regressors. This is a restricted maximum
28:1094:86036:11:0:91:523:95:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:likelihood estimator (REML). Unfortunately, the REML estimator does not exist for multilevel probit models.  AM use
29:1095:86153:0:19:91:523:732:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f4:B:justments across these error components to remove this downward bias in Online Appendix B3.2.27
29:1096:86248:19:16:107:523:713:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:In the case of the log wage outcome,vicontains the age 25 or 26 error component and thus
29:1097:86336:16:17:91:523:697:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:includes both permanent and transitory variation. We lack the wage panel data necessary to isolate
29:1098:86434:17:16:91:523:680:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the permanent component.  Consequently, we estimate the variance of the permanent component
29:1099:86525:16:17:91:523:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ofviunder the assumption that the transitory component of the wage makes up the same 52.3%
29:1100:86615:17:16:91:523:647:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:share of cross-sectional wage variance at age 25 in the ELS and NELS samples as it did for the high
29:1101:86714:16:19:91:491:631:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school class of 1972 cohort (sampled in NLS72) examined in Altonji and Mansfield (2011).
29:1102:86802:19:17:107:523:612:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The standard errors in the paper are based on a bootstrap approach encompassing the entire
29:1103:86892:17:16:91:524:595:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:estimation procedure, including the construction ofXXXs, imputation of missing data, and the bias
29:1104:86989:16:17:91:523:579:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:corrections to the standard deviations of the regression indices.  In particular, they account for the
29:1105:87091:17:16:91:523:562:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f4:B:fact that the coefficients that define the index variables used in the second step are estimated.28
29:1106:87190:16:17:91:523:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Across outcome variables,  data sets,  and specifications,  we find that the means of the bootstrap
29:1107:87289:17:16:91:523:529:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:replications of̂sd(XXXnGGGN1))),̂sd(XXXsGGGSs))),̂sd(ZZZS2sGGGS2))),̂sd(ZZZC2cGGGC2)))are above the point estimates.  Not
29:1108:87410:16:16:91:523:513:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:surprisingly, the disparity is even greater for the variance component estimates based on them, which
29:1109:87511:16:17:91:523:497:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:underlie the variance decompositions in Tables 2 and 3. The distribution of the bootstrap estimates
29:1110:87610:17:16:91:523:480:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:display a right skew as well as a mean shift. For this reason, we report 5thand 95thpercentile values
29:1111:87711:16:17:91:523:464:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the bootstrap distribution of our estimated variance components. The distribution of the estimates
29:1112:87812:17:16:91:524:447:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the treatment effects of a 10th-90thpercentile shift in school and/or commuting zone quality are
29:1113:87911:16:17:91:523:431:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:less sensitive to the issue.  More work on the best way to implement bias corrections and perform
29:1114:88008:17:33:91:254:414:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:related statistical inference is needed.
29:1115:88048:33:23:91:312:381:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:6.3    Variance Decomposition Methodology
29:1116:88089:23:17:107:523:358:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Here we describe the simple variance decomposition procedure that we use to 1) provide an
29:1117:88178:17:16:91:523:341:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:initial, descriptive sense of the relative importance of inputs at each level (individual, neighborhood,
29:1118:88282:16:17:91:523:325:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school, commuting zone) in determining the outcomes of interest, and 2) assess the degree to which
29:1119:88380:17:37:91:497:308:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:amenities at the neighborhood, school, and commuting zone level are driving student sorting.
29:1120:88472:37:11:91:523:271:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:the ML estimator for the two level random effects probit and then correct for bias based on the formulas relating the
29:1121:88589:11:11:91:523:260:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:unbiased estimators and the ML estimators in the case of a continuous outcome. We explored the possibility of extending
29:1122:88708:11:11:91:523:249:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:AM’s approach to the four level case, but were not able to find (or derive) the necessary formulas for four-level ML and
29:1123:88828:11:8:91:232:238:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:REML variance component estimators.
29:1124:88863:8:14:103:523:230:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:27If the bias-adjusted estimate of a variance is negative, we set it to 0.  The bias-adjusted estimate of the covariance
29:1125:88983:14:11:91:523:216:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:between two terms is set to 0 if the estimate of the variance of one of the terms is 0. If the correlation between two terms
29:1126:89107:11:11:91:523:205:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:implied by bias-adjusted estimates exceeds 1 in absolute value, we adjust the covariance to make the correlation 1 in
29:1127:89224:11:8:91:145:194:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:absolute value.
29:1128:89239:8:14:103:523:186:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:28The standard error estimates are based on re-sampling commuting zones with replacement.  To preserve the size
29:1129:89350:14:11:91:523:172:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:distribution of the samples of students from particular commuting zones, we divide the sample into ten CZ sample size
29:1130:89467:11:11:91:523:161:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:classes and resample CZs within class. For CZs in the largest size class, we break the CZs into two groups, each containing
29:1131:89590:11:11:91:523:150:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:half the schools.  We sample these half CZs instead to prevent any one bootstrap cluster from accounting for too large a
29:1132:89710:11:11:91:523:139:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:share of the sample.  Due to the considerable computational burden of the model estimation and the simulations relative
29:1133:89829:11:11:91:523:128:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:to the computer resources available for use with the restricted-use versions of ELS and NELS, we use 200 replications
29:1134:89946:11:11:91:523:117:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:to form the bootstrap estimates.  Furthermore, we use fewer (8) MCMC chains of length 500 rather than the 30 chains
29:1135:90061:11:11:91:523:106:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:of length 1000 that we use to compute the point estimates after first verifying that estimates are not very sensitive to this
29:1136:90186:11:0:91:329:95:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:choice. This might lead to an overstatement of the standard errors.
30:1137:90255:0:16:107:523:732:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:To simplify the empirical analysis we define neighborhoods to be nested within schools, which
30:1138:90348:16:17:91:524:716:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:are themselves nested within commuting zones.29We use a version of our estimating equation with
30:1139:90443:17:29:91:218:699:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f4:B:interaction terms excluded.30
30:1140:90472:29:33:116:523:670:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f4:A:Yi=XXXiBBB+XXXnGGGN1+XXXsGGGS1+ZZZS2sGGGS2+ZZZC2cGGGC2+(vi−vn)+(vn−vs)+(vs−vc)+vc.(26)
30:1141:90558:33:30:107:415:637:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:One can then write the outcome as the sum of orthogonal components:
30:1142:90625:30:32:217:523:607:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f4:A:Yi= (Yi−Yn)+(Yn−Ys)+(Ys−Yc)+Yc.(27)
30:1143:90660:32:30:107:405:575:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Because the components in (27) are mutually orthogonal,Var(Yi)is:
30:1144:90725:30:32:169:523:545:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f4:A:Var(Yi) =Var(Yi−Yn)+Var(Yn−Ys)+Var(Ys−Yc)+Var(Yc)(28)
30:1145:90778:32:17:107:523:513:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We are not able to fully characterize sorting on both observed and unobserved student level char-
30:1146:90875:17:16:91:524:496:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:acteristics because group averages ofXXXiabsorb not only the unobserved group averages ofXXXUibut
30:1147:90972:16:17:91:523:480:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:also part of the group treatment effect. Instead, we use an analogous four-component decomposition
30:1148:91070:17:16:91:524:463:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to analyze the structure of sorting on the index of observable characteristicsXXXiBBBthat best predicts
30:1149:91173:16:30:91:148:447:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the outcome:
30:1150:91185:30:30:114:523:417:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f4:A:Var(XXXiBBB) =Var((((XXXi−−−XXXn)))BBB)+Var((((XXXn−−−XXXs)))BBB)+Var((((XXXs−−−XXXc)))BBB)+Var(XXXcBBB)(29)
30:1151:91293:30:16:91:523:387:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:To our knowledge,  we are the first to perform such a decomposition based on commuting zone,
30:1152:91385:16:33:91:289:371:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school and census block group combinations.
30:1153:91428:33:24:91:477:338:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:6.4    Measuring the Effect of Shifts in School and Commuting Zone Quality
30:1154:91502:24:16:107:523:314:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Decompositions  of  variance  provide  an  overall  sense  of  the  relative  importance  of  individ-
30:1155:91604:16:17:91:523:298:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ual, neighborhood, school, and commuting zone inputs in determining later educational outcomes.
30:1156:91699:17:16:91:523:281:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:However, they do not permit one to easily gauge the impact that a substantial improvement in exter-
30:1157:91798:16:16:91:523:265:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:nal environment can have on a student’s expected educational attainment. Consequently, we extend
30:1158:91894:16:17:91:523:249:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to models with student-specific slopes AM’s methodology for converting variance components into
30:1159:91989:17:16:91:523:232:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:expected impacts on outcomes of particular shifts in school and/or community input quality.  Here
30:1160:92086:16:19:91:324:216:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we describe the three distinct treatments we consider.
30:1161:92140:19:14:103:523:197:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:29In the empirical work our narrowest definition of neighborhood is the census block group. In a few cases, students
30:1162:92256:14:11:91:523:183:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:from the same block group choose different schools.  We treat students from the same block group who attend different
30:1163:92373:11:11:91:523:172:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:schools as if they live in different block groups by using school-block group combinations as our definition of a neigh-
30:1164:92493:11:11:91:523:161:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:borhood.  Nesting neighborhood within school is required by the routine we use to estimate the variances of the error
30:1165:92610:11:11:91:523:150:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:components.  But it may diminish the precision of our estimates of neighborhood and school effects on outcomes.  The
30:1166:92726:11:11:91:523:139:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:same issue arises when ZIP code is the neighborhood definition. Note that the choice model in Section 2 allows families
30:1167:92845:11:8:91:312:128:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:who live in a given neighborhood to choose different schools.
30:1168:92906:8:14:103:523:120:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:30When interactions are included in the model, the within-neighborhood variance will differ across neighborhoods
30:1169:93018:14:11:91:523:106:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:based  on  the  degree  to  which  students  sensitive  to  environment  are  located  in  neighborhoods/schools  offering  more
30:1170:93146:11:0:91:311:95:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:supportive environments, preventing a simple decomposition.
31:1171:93207:0:23:91:383:732:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:C:6.4.1    The Combined School and Commuting Zone Treatment
31:1172:93264:23:17:107:523:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:First, we evaluate the expected change in outcomes associated with moving a randomly cho-
31:1173:93353:17:16:91:523:692:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:sen  student  from  a  school/commuting  zone  combination  at  the  10thpercentile  of  the  combined
31:1174:93455:16:17:91:523:676:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school/commuting zone quality distribution to the 90thpercentile. From our production function (8),
31:1175:93554:17:16:91:523:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the true distribution of combined school/commuting zone quality may be defined as the distribution
31:1176:93652:16:17:91:524:643:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ofZZZS∗sΓΓΓS∗+ZZZC∗cΓΓΓC∗.  We approximate this combined school/commuting zone quality distribution
31:1177:93751:17:16:91:523:626:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:using the distribution ofT=ZZZS2sGGGS2+ZZZC2cGGGC2+(vs−vc)+vc, where we useTto denote the particu-
31:1178:93849:16:17:91:524:610:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:lar “treatment” chosen. Because we exclude the control functionXXXnGGGN1+XXXsGGGS1, which may capture
31:1179:93950:17:16:91:523:593:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:peer effects and other unobserved school and commuting zone inputs in addition to student sort-
31:1180:94045:16:16:91:524:577:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ing, our estimated impacts of 10th-to-90thpercentile shifts in school/commuting zone quality will
31:1181:94142:16:17:91:524:561:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:likely understate the impact of the corresponding 10th-to-90thpercentile shifts inZZZS∗sΓΓΓS∗+ZZZC∗cΓΓΓC∗.
31:1182:94248:17:16:91:523:544:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:On the other hand, as the discussion in subsection 4.5.1 made clear, to the extent that unobserved
31:1183:94346:16:17:91:523:528:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:neighborhood inputs are clustered in particular schools and commuting zones, such inputs could
31:1184:94440:17:19:91:259:511:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f4:B:contribute toGGGS2,GGGC2,vs−vc, andvc.31
31:1185:94480:19:16:107:523:492:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Building an estimator of the impact of these quantile shift “treatments” is complicated by the
31:1186:94574:16:17:91:523:476:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f6:B:interaction terms in (10).  First, we assume that the treatment distributionT≡ZZZS2sGGGS2+ZZZC2cGGGC2+
31:1187:94676:17:16:91:523:459:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f4:B:(vs−vc) +vcis normally distributed, so that theq-th treatment quantile (denotedTq) is given by
31:1188:94770:16:17:91:524:443:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f4:B:Tq=ˆVar(T).5Φ−1(q)whereΦ−1(∗)is the inverse CDF of the standard normal distribution. Next,

32:1238:96763:17:16:91:524:680:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:student from a 10th-to-90thpercentile shift in school/commuting zone quality.  Alternatively, this

32:1243:97228:32:24:91:401:582:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:C:6.4.2    The School Treatment and the Commuting Zone Treatment
32:1244:97290:24:16:107:523:558:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The school counterfactual thought experiment or “treatment” consists of replacing each student’s
32:1245:97386:16:17:91:524:542:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:school inputs with those of the school at the 10thpercentile versus 90thpercentile of the school
32:1246:97482:17:16:91:523:525:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:quality distribution (defined byZZZSsΓΓΓS∗), holding neighborhood and commuting zone inputs fixed.
32:1247:97580:16:17:91:524:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We approximate the distribution of true school quality with the distribution ofT≡ZZZS2sGGGS2+(vs−vc).
32:1248:97681:17:16:91:524:492:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:This variable excludes the peer effects componentsXXXsΓΓΓS1andXXXUsΓΓΓSU1and also part ofZZZSU2sΓΓΓSU2sand
32:1249:97787:16:16:91:523:476:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:so is likely to understate school effects.   But as we discussed in 4.5.1, it may capture unobserved
32:1250:97887:16:20:91:352:460:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:neighborhood inputs that are clustered in particular schools.
32:1251:97948:20:16:107:523:440:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The commuting zone counterfactual “treatment” replaces each student’s commuting zone inputs
32:1252:98039:16:17:91:524:424:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:with  those  of  the  commuting  zone  at  the  10thpercentile  versus  90thpercentile  of  the  CZ  qual-
32:1253:98145:17:16:91:524:407:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ity  distribution  defined  byZZZCcΓΓΓC∗,  holding  neighborhood  and  school  inputs  fixed.   Importantly,
32:1254:98253:16:16:91:523:391:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:this treatment does not include the subcomponent of school inputs that varies across commuting
32:1255:98347:16:17:91:523:375:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:zones (given by the commuting zone average ofZZZS∗sΓΓΓS∗).  Thus, this counterfactual is designed to
32:1256:98447:17:16:91:523:358:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:gauge the importance of commuting zone-level inputs in their own right,  rather than the impor-
32:1257:98542:16:17:91:523:342:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:tance of the choice of commuting zone in which to live (which combines commuting zone inputs
32:1258:98634:17:16:91:523:325:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:with differences in the distributions of neighborhood and school inputs across commuting zones).
32:1259:98730:16:17:91:523:309:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We approximate the distribution of true commuting zone qualityZZZCcΓΓΓC∗with the distribution of
32:1260:98826:17:16:91:523:292:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f4:B:T≡ZZZC2cGGGC2+vc(and we do not include the between-CZ component ofZZZS2sGGGS2, sinceGGGS2is identified
32:1261:98928:16:16:91:524:276:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:using between-school/within-CZ variation and is designed to capture school effects).32This is again
32:1262:99027:16:17:91:523:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:an approximation due to the possibility thatZZZC2cGGGC2andvccould partly reflect unobserved school or
32:1263:99128:17:16:91:523:243:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:neighborhood inputs.  We refer to this counterfactual as the “CZ only” counterfactual in our tables
32:1264:99227:16:20:91:158:227:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and discussion.
32:1265:99242:20:16:107:523:207:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Computation of the school and CZ treatments are similar to the combined school and CZ treat-
32:1266:99334:16:71:91:309:191:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ment. The formulas are in Online Appendix B3.3.
32:1267:99381:71:14:103:523:120:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:32Recall that the commuting zone average ofZZZS2sGGGS2enters our estimating equation as a separate commuting zone
32:1268:99494:14:11:91:523:106:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:variable with its own coefficient. Because the coefficient on this index is identified purely from between-commuting zone
32:1269:99615:11:0:91:354:95:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:variation, we include this index as part of our commuting zone treatment.

33:1300:101959:20:16:107:523:286:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:As we discuss in detail in Section 8.1.1, part of the differential sensitivity for binary outcomes

34:1324:103928:17:19:91:284:471:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:estimates. These are not reported in Table 2.
34:1325:103973:19:16:107:523:452:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The main finding across data sets and outcomes is that individual-level factors account for the
34:1326:104068:16:17:91:523:436:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:lion’s share of the variance, with school and commuting zone level variation making modest con-
34:1327:104163:17:16:91:523:419:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:tributions,  and neighborhood level factors playing a smaller role.   In the case of HSGRAD, the
34:1328:104259:16:17:91:523:403:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:individual-level component accounts for 81.9% [77.5,  85.0] of the outcome variance for NELS,
34:1329:104352:17:16:91:523:386:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:81.6%  [75.2,  85.0]  for  the  ELS  block  group  specification,  and  85.6%  [77.2,  87.1]  for  the  ELS
34:1330:104459:16:16:91:523:370:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ZIP code specification.  The percentage of variance at the individual level is similar for wages and
34:1331:104559:16:17:91:523:354:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:slightly lower for college graduation. Interestingly, individual level factors are somewhat lower for
34:1332:104660:17:16:91:523:337:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:enrollment in a four-year college, averaging 72.3% across NELS and the two ELS specifications.
34:1333:104754:16:20:91:289:321:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f4:B:We do not have a good explanation for this.34
34:1334:104799:20:16:107:523:301:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The school level accounts for a substantial fraction of the variance in education outcomes. The
34:1335:104894:16:17:91:523:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:average of the NELS and the ELS estimates is 9.7% for high school graduation, 15.9% for 4 year
34:1336:104988:17:16:91:523:268:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:enrollment, and 10.8% for COLLBA. For wages, the estimates are about 9.0% for ELS but only
34:1337:105078:16:19:91:162:252:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:3.1% for NELS.
34:1338:105092:19:17:107:523:233:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Variation at the commuting zone level accounts for between 3.9% and 6.6 of the latent variable
34:1339:105186:17:16:91:523:216:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for high school graduation, about 10% for college enrollment, between 6.2% and 7.9% for college
34:1340:105281:16:19:91:313:200:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:graduation and between 6.2% and 7.6% for wages.
34:1341:105328:19:17:107:523:181:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We were surprised to find that differences across neighborhoods among students attending the
34:1342:105420:17:16:91:523:164:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:same schools do not account for much variation, even at the census block level. In the ZIP code case,
34:1343:105521:16:17:91:523:148:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the percentage varies between 0.6% and 1.3% across education outcomes. For wages the estimates
34:1344:105615:17:14:103:523:131:7:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:34It bears repeating that we are not using sampling weights due to computational difficulties in the second step and
34:1345:105731:14:11:91:523:117:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:so are not accounting for differences in designs of the ELS and NELS samples.  This could contribute to differences in
34:1346:105849:11:11:91:523:106:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:the estimates across ELS and NELS, although we noted earlier that the first step estimates of parameters involving the
34:1347:105967:11:0:91:243:95:9:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f4:F:observables are not sensitive to weighting.
35:1348:106012:0:16:91:523:732:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:are 0.7% for NELS and 1.1% for ELS. The ELS block group estimates are larger:  2.2% for HS-
35:1349:106103:16:17:91:523:716:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:GRAD, 3.1% for ENROLL, and 2.4% for COLLBA and 3.2% for wages. While the relatively small
35:1350:106192:17:16:91:523:699:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:neighborhood component is surprising, this does not necessarily mean that neighborhood effects
35:1351:106286:16:16:91:523:683:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and neighborhood-level sorting are unimportant.  Alternatively, the high and low quality neighbor-
35:1352:106384:16:17:91:523:667:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:hoods might be sufficiently grouped within school attendance zones that most of the variation in
35:1353:106480:17:16:91:523:650:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:neighborhood inputs is across schools. As discussed in sections 4.5.1 and 6.3, the variance decom-
35:1354:106578:16:17:91:523:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:positions are crafted to prevent higher-level inputs (eg., commuting zone) from being reflected in
35:1355:106676:17:16:91:523:617:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:lower-level variance components, but between-school variation in neighborhood quality will still be
35:1356:106775:16:17:91:523:601:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:captured by the between-school variance components.  This may be reinforced by the fact that the
35:1357:106871:17:16:91:523:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ELS and NELS samples are clustered at the school level rather than at the neighborhood level.  In
35:1358:106968:16:16:91:523:568:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:addition, ZIP code may be too coarse a measure of neighborhood, with many ZIP codes containing
35:1359:107062:16:17:91:523:552:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a mix of both good and bad neighborhoods (so that the bulk of neighborhood variation is assigned
35:1360:107158:17:16:91:523:535:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to the “within-neighborhood” category in Table 2). But the estimates are still relatively small when
35:1361:107258:16:33:91:404:519:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we use block group. Clustering is clearly much stronger across schools.
35:1362:107329:33:16:91:523:486:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:F:7.1.2    The  Extent  of  Student  Sorting  at  the  Neighborhood,  School,  and  Commuting  Zone
35:1363:107426:16:24:124:153:470:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:C:Levels

39:1547:120472:16:16:91:523:500:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:group, and ELS ZIP code results are denoted by the column headings NELS, ELSbg, and ELSz

39:1550:120675:16:17:91:523:448:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:zone increases the high school graduation probability from 0.816 to 0.904,  an increase of 0.088
39:1551:120771:17:16:91:523:431:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(0.014).   This is very large relative to the dropout rate.   A corresponding shift in the school en-
39:1552:120872:16:16:91:523:415:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:vironment only (while receiving a random draw from the distribution of commuting zone inputs)
39:1553:120965:16:17:91:523:399:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:changes the graduation probability from 0.831 to 0.891, an increase of 0.06 (0.014). The effect of a

39:1555:121134:19:17:107:523:363:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The estimates for ELS are somewhat smaller.  In the ELS block group case, the “School and
39:1556:121223:17:16:91:523:346:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:CZ” treatment raises the graduation probability by 0.062 (0.011), from 0.896 to 0.958.  The school

39:1560:121615:16:17:91:523:281:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:0.92.  Thus, there is not much room for increases in the graduation probability.  Online Appendix
39:1561:121712:17:16:91:523:264:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Table B7 displays estimates of the impacts of 10th-to-50thquantile shifts for the same treatments.
39:1562:121810:16:17:91:523:248:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Despite the nonlinearity in the probit function, the estimates are only slightly more than half as large
39:1563:121914:17:16:91:523:231:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:as the corresponding 10th-to-90thshifts, so the shift from the 50th-to-90thquantile matters almost as
39:1564:122015:16:19:91:300:215:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:much as the shift from the 10th-to-50thquantile.
39:1565:122063:19:17:107:523:196:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The estimates for the combined “School and CZ” treatment for ELS are essentially identical to
39:1566:122156:17:16:91:523:179:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the school-level estimates from the full specification in AM that implicitly incorporate CZ inputs as
39:1567:122257:16:17:91:523:163:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:well. The NELS estimates are about .04 smaller than the corresponding estimates in AM, indicating
39:1568:122354:17:16:91:523:146:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:some sensitivity to the changes in specification introduced in this paper:  the exclusion of weights,
39:1569:122455:16:17:91:523:130:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:the addition of interactions, and the addition of neighborhood-level control function variablesXn

40:1571:122583:0:16:107:523:732:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:While the estimates are somewhat noisy, taken together the results for HSGRAD indicate that
40:1572:122674:16:17:91:523:716:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:large shifts in school and commuting zone inputs could generate socially significant impacts on
40:1573:122769:17:16:91:523:699:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f7:B:graduation probabilities.  Note that this is true even though the components(ZZZS2sGGGS2,ZZZC2cGGGC2,(vs−
40:1574:122874:16:16:91:524:683:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f4:B:vc),vc)that we ascribe to school and commuting zone inputs comprise relatively small fractions of
40:1575:122971:16:17:91:523:667:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f6:B:the outcome variance. Row 8 of Appendix Table A1 reports that the variance ofZZZS2sGGGS2+ZZZC2cGGGC2+
40:1576:123072:17:16:91:523:650:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f4:B:(vs−vc) +vc, which drives the “School and CZ” treatment, comprises just 2.2 percent of the full
40:1577:123167:16:17:91:523:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:latent index variance in NELS, and only 3.0 percent in the ELS block group specification.37Rows 9
40:1578:123264:17:16:91:523:617:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and 10 report that the school inputs that matter for the “School Only” treatment and the commuting
40:1579:123362:16:17:91:523:601:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:zone inputs that matter for the “CZ Only” treatment each account for only about 1 to 1.5 percent of
40:1580:123461:17:19:91:241:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the latent index variance in NELS.
40:1581:123495:19:16:107:523:565:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Why do 10th-to-90thquantile shifts in distributions featuring such small variances generate such
40:1582:123591:16:17:91:523:549:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:substantial outcome changes? One reason is that variance components, by virtue of squaring devia-
40:1583:123688:17:16:91:523:532:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:tions, tend to exacerbate differences in the relative importance of various inputs; standard deviations
40:1584:123791:16:17:91:523:516:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of these components are considerably closer in size to those corresponding to individual-level in-

43:1707:133409:17:16:91:523:650:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:normal distribution of unobserved inputs, so that an outcome mean of .5 suggests that many students
